<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">2016 Atlantic hurricane season</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<a href="/wiki/2014_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2014 Atlantic hurricane season">2014</a>, <a href="/wiki/2015_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2015 Atlantic hurricane season">2015</a>, <b>2016</b>, <a href="/wiki/2017_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2017 Atlantic hurricane season">2017</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_naming" title="Tropical cyclone naming">2018</a></i></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>2016 Atlantic hurricane season</b> was the most active, costliest, and deadliest <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="Atlantic hurricane season">hurricane season</a> since <a href="/wiki/2012_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2012 Atlantic hurricane season">2012</a>. The season officially started on June 1 and ended on November 30, though the first storm, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Alex_(2016)" title="Hurricane Alex (2016)">Hurricane Alex</a> which formed in the Northeastern Atlantic, developed on January 12, being the first hurricane to develop in January since <a href="/wiki/1938_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1938 Atlantic hurricane season">1938</a>. The final storm, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Otto" title="Hurricane Otto">Otto</a>, crossed into the Eastern Pacific on November 25, a few days before the official end. Following Alex, <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Bonnie_(2016)" title="Tropical Storm Bonnie (2016)">Tropical Storm Bonnie</a> brought flooding to <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> and portions of <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Colin_(2016)" title="Tropical Storm Colin (2016)">Tropical Storm Colin</a> in early June brought minor flooding and wind damage to parts of the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States" title="Southeastern United States">Southeastern United States</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Earl_(2016)" title="Hurricane Earl (2016)">Hurricane Earl</a> left 94 fatalities in the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, 81 of which occurred in the latter. In early September, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Hermine" title="Hurricane Hermine">Hurricane Hermine</a>, the first hurricane to make landfall in Florida since <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Wilma" title="Hurricane Wilma">Hurricane Wilma</a> in <a href="/wiki/2005_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2005 Atlantic hurricane season">2005</a>, brought extensive coastal flooding damage especially to the <a href="/wiki/Forgotten_Coast" title="Forgotten Coast">Forgotten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nature_Coast" title="Nature Coast">Nature</a> coasts of Florida. Hermine was responsible for five fatalities and about $550 million (2016 <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">USD</a>) in damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-damage_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-damage-2">[b]</a></sup></p>
<p>The strongest, costliest, and deadliest storm of the season was <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Matthew" title="Hurricane Matthew">Hurricane Matthew</a>, the southernmost <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Category_5_Atlantic_hurricane" title="Category 5 Atlantic hurricane">Category 5 Atlantic hurricane</a> on record and the first to reach that intensity since <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Felix" title="Hurricane Felix">Felix</a> in <a href="/wiki/2007_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2007 Atlantic hurricane season">2007</a>. With at least 603 deaths attributed to it, Matthew was the deadliest Atlantic hurricane since <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Stan" title="Hurricane Stan">Stan</a> of <a href="/wiki/2005_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2005 Atlantic hurricane season">2005</a>. Furthermore, damage from Matthew is estimated to be at least $15.1 billion, making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_costliest_Atlantic_hurricanes" title="List of costliest Atlantic hurricanes">ninth costliest Atlantic hurricane</a> on record. <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Nicole_(2016)" title="Hurricane Nicole (2016)">Hurricane Nicole</a> became the first major hurricane to directly impact <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a> since <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Fabian" title="Hurricane Fabian">Hurricane Fabian</a> in <a href="/wiki/2003_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2003 Atlantic hurricane season">2003</a>, leaving widespread but relatively minor damage on the island. The final tropical cyclone of the season – Hurricane Otto – brought severe flooding to <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> in November, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>. Otto left 23 deaths and about $190 million in damage. On November 25, the storm <a href="/wiki/List_of_Atlantic%E2%80%93Pacific_crossover_hurricanes" title="List of Atlantic–Pacific crossover hurricanes">emerged into the Eastern Pacific basin</a>, the first such occurrence since <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Cesar%E2%80%93Douglas" title="Hurricane Cesar–Douglas">Hurricane Cesar–Douglas</a> in <a href="/wiki/1996_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1996 Atlantic hurricane season">1996</a>. Most of the season's tropical cyclones impacted land, and nine of those storms caused loss of life. Collectively, the storms left at least 743 fatalities and $16.1 billion in damage.</p>
<p>Most forecasting groups predicted above average activity in anticipation of a dissipating <a href="/wiki/2014%E2%80%9316_El_Ni%C3%B1o_event" title="2014–16 El Niño event">El Niño event</a> and the development of a <a href="/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a" title="La Niña">La Niña</a>, as well as warmer than normal <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">sea surface temperatures</a>. Overall, the forecasts were fairly accurate.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Seasonal_forecasts">Seasonal forecasts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Seasonal forecasts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table cellpadding="3" cellspacing="0" class="toccolours" style="float:right; margin-left:1em; text-align:right; clear: right;">
<caption><b>Predictions of tropical activity in the 2016 season</b></caption>
<tr style="background:#ccf; text-align:center;">
<td><b>Source</b></td>
<td><b>Date</b></td>
<td><b><span style="font-size: 80%;">Named<br/>
storms</span></b></td>
<td><b><span style="font-size: 80%;">Hurricanes</span></b></td>
<td><b><span style="font-size: 80%;">Major<br/>
hurricanes</span></b></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><i>Average <span style="font-size: 80%;">(1981–2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Background_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Background-3">[1]</a></sup>)</span></i></td>

<p>Ahead of and during the season, several meteorological services and scientific agencies forecast how many named storms, hurricanes and major hurricanes will form during a season and/or how many tropical cyclones will affect a particular country. These agencies include the Tropical Storm Risk (TSR) Consortium of the University College London, the <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> (NOAA), <a href="/wiki/Met_Office" title="Met Office">United Kingdom Met Office</a> (UKMO), <a href="/wiki/Coastal_Carolina_University" title="Coastal Carolina University">Coastal Carolina University</a> (CCU), <a href="/wiki/Colorado_State_University" title="Colorado State University">Colorado State University</a> (CSU), and <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_State_University" title="North Carolina State University">North Carolina State University</a> (NCSU). The forecasts include weekly and monthly changes in significant factors that help determine the number of tropical storms, hurricanes, and major hurricanes within a particular year. Some of these forecasts also take into consideration what happened in previous seasons and the predicted weakening of the <a href="/wiki/2014%E2%80%9316_El_Ni%C3%B1o_event" title="2014–16 El Niño event">2014–16 El Niño event</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dec16TSR_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dec16TSR-4">[2]</a></sup> On average, an Atlantic hurricane season between 1981 and 2010 contained twelve tropical storms, six hurricanes, and two major hurricanes, with an <a href="/wiki/Accumulated_cyclone_energy" title="Accumulated cyclone energy">accumulated cyclone energy</a> (ACE) index of between 66 and 103 units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Background_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Background-3">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-season_forecasts">Pre-season forecasts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-season forecasts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first forecast for the year was issued by CSU on December 11, who anticipated that one of four different scenarios could occur. The scenario considered most likely was that <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_multidecadal_oscillation" title="Atlantic multidecadal oscillation">Atlantic multidecadal oscillation</a> (AMO) and <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> (THC) would be stronger, but effects from El Niño would remain, resulting in a slightly above average season. The next most likely scenario was that both the AMO and THC would strengthen and the El Niño effects would cease to exist, causing a well above average season. In the other two scenarios, which were given the same probability of occurrence, the AMO and THC would weaken and the effects of El Niño would either disappear or some would remain, resulting in either a near average or well below average season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dec15CSU_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dec15CSU-18">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>TSR subsequently issued their first outlook for the 2016 season during December 16, 2015 and predicted that activity would be about 20% below the 1950–2015 average, or about 15% below the 2005–2015 average. Specifically they thought that there would be 13 tropical storms, 5 hurricanes, 2 major hurricanes and an ACE index of 79 units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dec16TSR_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dec16TSR-4">[2]</a></sup> A few months later, TSR issued their second prediction for the season during April 6, 2016 and lowered the predicted number of named storms to 12 but raised the number of hurricanes to 6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apr16TSR_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Apr16TSR-5">[3]</a></sup> On April 14, CSU predicted that the season would be near-normal, predicting 13 named storms, 6 hurricanes and 2 major hurricanes with ACE near 93.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSUforecast_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-CSUforecast-6">[4]</a></sup> On April 15, NCSU predicted the season would be very active, with 15–18 named storms, 8–11 hurricanes and 3–5 major hurricanes. A month later, the UKMO released its forecast, predicting a slightly above-average season with 14 named storms and 8 hurricanes. It also predicted an ACE index of 125, above the defined average ACE index at 103.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UKMOforecast_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-UKMOforecast-9">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 27, NOAA issued its first outlook calling for a near-normal season with a 70% chance that 10–16 named storms could form, including 4–8 hurricanes of which 1–4 could reach major hurricane status. NOAA also stated that there is a 45% chance of a near-normal season, 30% chance of an above-normal season and 25% chance of a below-normal season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaamay27_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-noaamay27-10">[8]</a></sup> Also on May 27, TSR substantially increased their forecast numbers, predicting activity would be about 30% above the average with 17 named storms, 9 hurricanes, 4 major hurricanes and an ACE near 130. The reason for the increased activity forecast was the increased likelihood of <a href="/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a" title="La Niña">La Niña</a> forming during the season in addition to a trend towards a negative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Oscillation" title="North Atlantic Oscillation">North Atlantic Oscillation</a>, which generally favors a warmer tropical Atlantic. TSR predicted that there is a 57% chance that the 2016 Atlantic season would be above-normal, a 33% chance it would be near-normal, and only a 10% chance it would be below-normal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsrmay27_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-tsrmay27-11">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-season_outlooks">Mid-season outlooks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mid-season outlooks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>CSU updated their forecast on June 1 to include 14 named storms, 6 hurricanes and 2 major hurricanes to include Tropical Storm Bonnie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csujune1_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-csujune1-12">[10]</a></sup> It was again updated on July 1 to include 15 named storms, 6 hurricanes and 2 major hurricanes, to accommodate for tropical storms Colin and Danielle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csujuly1_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-csujuly1-13">[11]</a></sup> On July 5, TSR released their fourth forecast for the season, slightly lowering the predicted numbers to 16 tropical storms, 8 hurricanes and 3 major hurricanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jul5TSR_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jul5TSR-14">[12]</a></sup> On August 5, TSR released their final forecast for the season, lowering the numbers to 15 named storms and 7 hurricanes due to the influence that La Niña being less than anticipated previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aug5TSR_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aug5TSR-16">[14]</a></sup> NOAA updated their forecast on August 11, increasing the their predictions to 12–17 named storms, 5–8 hurricanes, and 2–4 major hurricanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaaaugust11_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-noaaaugust11-17">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Seasonal_summary">Seasonal summary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Seasonal summary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="Timeline of the 2016 Atlantic hurricane season">Timeline of the 2016 Atlantic hurricane season</a></div>
<center>

</center>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_tropical_cyclones_Atlantic_2016-08-31.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="972" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Three_tropical_cyclones_Atlantic_2016-08-31.png/330px-Three_tropical_cyclones_Atlantic_2016-08-31.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Three_tropical_cyclones_Atlantic_2016-08-31.png/495px-Three_tropical_cyclones_Atlantic_2016-08-31.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Three_tropical_cyclones_Atlantic_2016-08-31.png/660px-Three_tropical_cyclones_Atlantic_2016-08-31.png 2x" width="330"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_tropical_cyclones_Atlantic_2016-08-31.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Three tropical cyclones active in the Atlantic basin on August 31. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Hermine" title="Hurricane Hermine">Hermine</a> <i>(left)</i>, <a href="#Tropical_Depression_Eight">TD Eight</a> <i>(northeast of Hermine, middle)</i>, and <a href="#Hurricane_Gaston">Gaston</a> <i>(right)</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 2016 Atlantic hurricane season officially began on June 1, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncsu_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ncsu-8">[6]</a></sup> It was an above average season and the most active since <a href="/wiki/2012_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2012 Atlantic hurricane season">2012</a>, producing a total of 15 named storms, 7 hurricanes, and 4 major hurricanes. The first storm, Hurricane Alex, developed on January 12, while the final system, Hurricane Otto, made a crossover to the Eastern Pacific on November 25. The higher-than-normal activity was attributed to many factors. Most significantly, one of the strongest El Niño events recorded in history rapidly dissipated, transforming to cool-neutral conditions across the Pacific in late summer. This led to warmer than normal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperatures" title="Sea surface temperatures">sea surface temperatures</a> across the Atlantic, though the subtropical regions were slightly cooler than normal; slightly lower than normal sea level pressures; and reduced wind shear, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a>, which had experienced record values of wind shear in the past recent years. Moisture levels, however, were anomalously dry, which likely prevented some of the storms from becoming significant hurricanes. Steering currents had also been different from past years, which had previously had a trough of low pressure dominating the <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="East Coast of the United States">East Coast of the United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSU_summary_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSU_summary-19">[17]</a></sup> The tropical cyclones of this season caused about $16.1 billion in damage and at least 743 deaths,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> being the costliest season since 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> The Atlantic hurricane season officially ended on November 30, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncsu_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-ncsu-8">[6]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Matthew_2016-10-02_1815Z.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Matthew_2016-10-02_1815Z.png/220px-Matthew_2016-10-02_1815Z.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Matthew_2016-10-02_1815Z.png/330px-Matthew_2016-10-02_1815Z.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Matthew_2016-10-02_1815Z.png/440px-Matthew_2016-10-02_1815Z.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Matthew_2016-10-02_1815Z.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hurricane Matthew near its secondary peak intensity northwest of <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> on October 2</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The year opened up with an anomalous storm in January: <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Alex_(2016)" title="Hurricane Alex (2016)">Hurricane Alex</a>, the first such system to develop in January since <a href="/wiki/1938_Atlantic_hurricane_season#Hurricane_One" title="1938 Atlantic hurricane season">1938</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlexTCR_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlexTCR-22">[20]</a></sup> Activity picked up at the end of May into June, with three consecutive tropical storms: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Bonnie_(2016)" title="Tropical Storm Bonnie (2016)">Bonnie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Colin_(2016)" title="Tropical Storm Colin (2016)">Colin</a>, and Danielle. The latter two were the earliest third- and fourth-named storms on record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BonnieTCR_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-BonnieTCR-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColinTCR_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColinTCR-24">[22]</a></sup> July saw no storm development for the first time in four years, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> August saw the formation of six tropical cyclones, including <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Earl_(2016)" title="Hurricane Earl (2016)">Earl</a>, <a href="#Tropical_Storm_Fiona">Fiona</a>, <a href="#Hurricane_Gaston">Gaston</a>, <a href="#Tropical_Depression_Eight">Eight</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Hermine" title="Hurricane Hermine">Hermine</a>. A Category 1 hurricane, Earl wrought tremendous damage in <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. With 81 lives lost in Mexico during the passage of Earl, it was the deadliest Atlantic hurricane in the country since 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EarlTCR_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-EarlTCR-26">[24]</a></sup> Gaston became the season's first <a href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_scale#Category_3" title="Saffir–Simpson scale">major hurricane</a> on August 28, attaining peak winds of 120 mph (195 km/h) over the central Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GastonTCR_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-GastonTCR-27">[25]</a></sup> On September 1, Hermine <a href="/wiki/Landfall" title="Landfall">struck</a> the <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida Peninsula</a> as a Category 1 hurricane, ending an 11-year drought of hurricane landfalls in the state, which began after <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Wilma" title="Hurricane Wilma">Hurricane Wilma</a> in October 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HermineTCR_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-HermineTCR-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>September featured another five tropical cyclones: <a href="#Tropical_Storm_Ian">Ian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Julia_(2016)" title="Tropical Storm Julia (2016)">Julia</a>, <a href="#Tropical_Storm_Karl">Karl</a>, <a href="#Tropical_Storm_Lisa">Lisa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Matthew" title="Hurricane Matthew">Matthew</a>, the latter of which persisted into October. Matthew proved to be the most significant storm of the season, becoming the first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Category_5_Atlantic_hurricanes" title="List of Category 5 Atlantic hurricanes">Category 5 hurricane</a> in the Atlantic since <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Felix" title="Hurricane Felix">Hurricane Felix</a> in <a href="/wiki/2007_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2007 Atlantic hurricane season">2007</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MatthewC5_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-MatthewC5-29">[27]</a></sup> and, with a death toll of over 600, it was the deadliest in the Atlantic basin since <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Stan" title="Hurricane Stan">Hurricane Stan</a> in <a href="/wiki/2005_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2005 Atlantic hurricane season">2005</a>. It subsequently struck <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> as a Category 4 hurricane, and inflicted catastrophic damage across the impoverished nation. Matthew also caused extensive damage in Cuba, the Bahamas, and the Southeastern United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthew_TCR_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matthew_TCR-30">[28]</a></sup> Concurrently, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Nicole_(2016)" title="Hurricane Nicole (2016)">Hurricane Nicole</a> meandered south of Bermuda for more than a week before making a direct hit on the territory as a major hurricane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicoleTCR_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicoleTCR-31">[29]</a></sup> The next four weeks were quiet, until <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Otto" title="Hurricane Otto">Hurricane Otto</a> formed in the southwestern Caribbean during late November. Otto eventually became the latest-forming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_hurricane" title="Major hurricane">major hurricane</a> in the Atlantic basin on record, surpassing a storm in <a href="/wiki/1934_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1934 Atlantic hurricane season">1934</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OttoTCR_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-OttoTCR-32">[30]</a></sup> After striking <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> and becoming the first hurricane on record to pass over <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, Otto – the final tropical cyclone of the season – then <a href="/wiki/List_of_Atlantic%E2%80%93Pacific_crossover_hurricanes" title="List of Atlantic–Pacific crossover hurricanes">emerged into the Eastern Pacific basin</a> on November 25, the first such occurrence since <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Cesar%E2%80%93Douglas" title="Hurricane Cesar–Douglas">Hurricane Cesar–Douglas</a> in <a href="/wiki/1996_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1996 Atlantic hurricane season">1996</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OttoTCR_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-OttoTCR-32">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The season's activity was reflected with an <a href="/wiki/Accumulated_cyclone_energy" title="Accumulated cyclone energy">accumulated cyclone energy</a> index of 141 units,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACE_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACE-33">[31]</a></sup> which was well above the 1981–2010 median of 92,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apr14CSU_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Apr14CSU-34">[32]</a></sup> as well as the highest value since <a href="/wiki/2010_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2010 Atlantic hurricane season">2010</a>. Broadly speaking, ACE is a measure of the power of a tropical or subtropical storm multiplied by the length of time it existed. Therefore, a storm with a longer duration or stronger intensity, such as Matthew, will have high values of ACE. It is only calculated for full advisories on specific tropical systems reaching or exceeding wind speeds of 39 mph (63 km/h). Accordingly, tropical depressions are not included here. After the storm has dissipated, typically after the end of the season, the NHC reexamines the data, and produces a final report on each storm. These revisions can lead to a revised ACE total either upward or downward compared to the operational value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACE_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-ACE-33">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Systems">Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hurricane_Alex">Hurricane Alex</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Hurricane Alex">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 24em;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight: bold; background: #ffffcc">Category 1 hurricane (SSHWS)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="vertical-align: middle"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alex_2016-01-14_1435Z.jpg"><img alt="Alex 2016-01-14 1435Z.jpg" data-file-height="4160" data-file-width="3120" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Alex_2016-01-14_1435Z.jpg/150px-Alex_2016-01-14_1435Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Alex_2016-01-14_1435Z.jpg/225px-Alex_2016-01-14_1435Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Alex_2016-01-14_1435Z.jpg/300px-Alex_2016-01-14_1435Z.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a><span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alex_2016_track.png"><img alt="Alex 2016 track.png" data-file-height="1945" data-file-width="2700" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Alex_2016_track.png/150px-Alex_2016_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Alex_2016_track.png/225px-Alex_2016_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Alex_2016_track.png/300px-Alex_2016_track.png 2x" width="150"/></a></span></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>January 12 – January 15</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Peak intensity</th>
<td>85 mph (140 km/h) <small>(1-min)</small>  981 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Alex_(2016)" title="Hurricane Alex (2016)">Hurricane Alex (2016)</a></div>
<p>A weak area of low pressure developed over northwestern Cuba in association with a <a href="/wiki/Stationary_front" title="Stationary front">stationary front</a> on January 6. The frontal wave intensified as it moved into the central Atlantic, temporarily attaining hurricane-force winds by January 10. Steered by anomalous high pressure, the disturbance turned southeast and tracked over warmer waters. Its associated fronts dissipated, its wind field became more symmetric, and convection increased near the center, leading to the formation of Subtropical Storm Alex by 18:00 UTC on January 12. Despite marginal ocean temperatures, Alex benefited from rapidly cooling upper-air temperatures, and it intensified quickly while turning northeast. The presence of deeper convection near the center and an <a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)" title="Eye (cyclone)">eye</a> on conventional satellite showcased the storm's transition into a fully tropical cyclone and intensification into a hurricane by 06:00 UTC on January 14. Six hours later, it peaked with maximum sustained winds of 85 mph (140 km/h). Alex turned north after peak, and the storm weakened to a tropical storm before making landfall on <a href="/wiki/Terceira_Island" title="Terceira Island">Terceira Island, Azores</a>. With decreasing core convection and an impinging warm front, Alex transitioned into an <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">extratropical cyclone</a> by 18:00 UTC on January 15 and was absorbed by a larger extratropical low two days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlexTCR_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-AlexTCR-22">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The precursor disturbance to Hurricane Alex produced gusts up to 60 mph (97 km/h) on Bermuda, as well as swells up to 20 ft (6 m) offshore; this disrupted air travel, downed trees, caused sporadic power outages, and suspended ferry services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> In the Azores, the cyclone produced maximum rainfall accumulations up to 4.04 in (103 mm) in <a href="/wiki/Lagoa,_Azores" title="Lagoa, Azores">Lagoa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> Peak gusts of 57 mph (92 km/h) affected <a href="/wiki/Ponta_Delgada" title="Ponta Delgada">Ponta Delgada</a>, causing minor to moderate damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> Landslides also contributed to minor damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> One death occurred when a victim that suffered a heart attack was unable to be airlifted to a hospital due to unsettled conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_Storm_Bonnie">Tropical Storm Bonnie</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Tropical Storm Bonnie">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 24em;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight: bold; background: #00faf4">Tropical storm (SSHWS)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="vertical-align: middle"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bonnie_2016-05-28_Suomi_NPP.jpg"><img alt="Bonnie 2016-05-28 Suomi NPP.jpg" data-file-height="2745" data-file-width="1957" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bonnie_2016-05-28_Suomi_NPP.jpg/150px-Bonnie_2016-05-28_Suomi_NPP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bonnie_2016-05-28_Suomi_NPP.jpg/225px-Bonnie_2016-05-28_Suomi_NPP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bonnie_2016-05-28_Suomi_NPP.jpg/300px-Bonnie_2016-05-28_Suomi_NPP.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a><span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bonnie_2016_track.png"><img alt="Bonnie 2016 track.png" data-file-height="1669" data-file-width="2700" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Bonnie_2016_track.png/150px-Bonnie_2016_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Bonnie_2016_track.png/225px-Bonnie_2016_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Bonnie_2016_track.png/300px-Bonnie_2016_track.png 2x" width="150"/></a></span></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>May 27 – June 4</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Peak intensity</th>
<td>45 mph (75 km/h) <small>(1-min)</small>  1006 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Bonnie_(2016)" title="Tropical Storm Bonnie (2016)">Tropical Storm Bonnie (2016)</a></div>
<p>An area of low pressure developed into Tropical Depression Two at 18:00 UTC on May 27, while situated about 205 mi (330 km) northeast of <a href="/wiki/Abaco_Islands" title="Abaco Islands">Great Abaco</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bahamas" title="Bahamas">Bahamas</a>. Moving steadily west-northwestwards, Bonnie intensified into a tropical storm on May 28. Shortly thereafter, the storm reached its peak winds of 45 mph (75 km/h). However, due to hostile environmental conditions, Bonnie weakened to a depression hours before making landfall just east of <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Palms,_South_Carolina" title="Isle of Palms, South Carolina">Isle of Palms, South Carolina</a>, on May 29. Steering currents collapsed afterwards, causing the storm to meander over <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> for two days. The storm weakened further into a non-tropical remnant low on May 31, before emerging off the coast while moving generally east-northeastwards. On June 2, Bonnie regenerated into a tropical depression just offshore <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a> as conditions became slightly more favorable. The next day, despite increasing wind shear and cooling sea surface temperatures, Bonnie reintensified into a tropical storm and reached its minimum barometric pressure of 1,006 mbar (29.7 inHg). The storm weakened to a tropical depression late on June 4 and became a non-tropical low again early the next day to the north of <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>. The remnants moved east-southeast until dissipating on June 9.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BonnieTCR_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-BonnieTCR-23">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rip currents along the coastline of the Southeast United States led to dozens of water rescues; the body of one 20-year-old man was recovered in <a href="/wiki/Brevard_County,_Florida" title="Brevard County, Florida">Brevard County, Florida</a>, after he drowned,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> Lingering over South Carolina for a few days, Bonnie brought heavy rains and widespread floods to the Southeastern United States. Rainfall totals hit 6 in (150 mm) in much of South Carolina, and exceeded 10 in (250 mm) in some areas. Flooding resulted in the closure of the southbound lanes of <a href="/wiki/Interstate_95" title="Interstate 95">Interstate 95</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jasper_County,_South_Carolina" title="Jasper County, South Carolina">Jasper County</a>, and also inundated the Jasper County Sheriff's Office and Detention Center. In <a href="/wiki/Ridgeland,_South_Carolina" title="Ridgeland, South Carolina">Ridgeland</a>, several buildings were damaged and the local wastewater treatment plant overflowed, spilling discharge into the nearby Captain Bill Creek. Damage in this county alone exceeded $640,000. Record-breaking rainfall was observed across much of the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Banks" title="Outer Banks">Outer Banks</a>; on <a href="/wiki/Hatteras_Island" title="Hatteras Island">Hatteras Island</a>, Cape Point Campground was closed for a week due to flooding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> In North Carolina, the body of a 21-year-old man was recovered in <a href="/wiki/New_Hanover_County,_North_Carolina" title="New Hanover County, North Carolina">New Hanover County</a>, several days after he went missing in rough surf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_Storm_Colin">Tropical Storm Colin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Tropical Storm Colin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 24em;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight: bold; background: #00faf4">Tropical storm (SSHWS)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="vertical-align: middle"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colin_2016-06-06_1620Z.jpg"><img alt="Colin 2016-06-06 1620Z.jpg" data-file-height="7200" data-file-width="5600" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Colin_2016-06-06_1620Z.jpg/150px-Colin_2016-06-06_1620Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Colin_2016-06-06_1620Z.jpg/225px-Colin_2016-06-06_1620Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Colin_2016-06-06_1620Z.jpg/300px-Colin_2016-06-06_1620Z.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a><span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colin_2016_track.png"><img alt="Colin 2016 track.png" data-file-height="2093" data-file-width="3000" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Colin_2016_track.png/150px-Colin_2016_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Colin_2016_track.png/225px-Colin_2016_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Colin_2016_track.png/300px-Colin_2016_track.png 2x" width="150"/></a></span></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>June 5 – June 7</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Peak intensity</th>
<td>50 mph (85 km/h) <small>(1-min)</small>  1001 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Colin_(2016)" title="Tropical Storm Colin (2016)">Tropical Storm Colin (2016)</a></div>
<p>On May 27, a tropical wave exited the coast of Africa. By early June, the wave entered the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a> and spawned a low pressure system. The low remained disorganized with only isolated convection, mostly in the eastern quadrant. Convection began to wrap into the center as the storm curved northward into the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> on June 3. After the low passed over the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a> on June 5, the <a href="/wiki/National_Hurricane_Center" title="National Hurricane Center">National Hurricane Center</a> (NHC) upgraded it to Tropical Depression Three. Later that day, the depression intensified into Tropical Storm Colin. Gradually curving northeastwards, Colin remained disorganized as it accelerated towards the coast of Florida on June 6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColinTCR_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-ColinTCR-24">[22]</a></sup> The NHC noted that there was uncertainty in locating the circulation center, instead taking the midpoint between two small-scale circulations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> However, the NHC increased the winds to 50 mph (85 km/h) following a strong burst in Colin's convection. Colin continued accelerating to the northeast and made landfall near <a href="/wiki/Keaton_Beach,_Florida" title="Keaton Beach, Florida">Keaton Beach, Florida</a>, at 02:00 UTC on June 7. Failing to weaken over land,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColinTCR_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-ColinTCR-24">[22]</a></sup> Colin began undergoing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extratropical_transition" title="Extratropical transition">extratropical transition</a> after the increasingly ill-defined circulation moved off the coast of Georgia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> and became fully extratropical hours later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColinTCR_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-ColinTCR-24">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Cuba, heavy rainfall resulted in flooding in the western portions of the island, especially <a href="/wiki/Pinar_del_R%C3%ADo_Province" title="Pinar del Río Province">Pinar del Río Province</a>. Water left several roads impassable and inundated crops in some areas;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cuba1_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cuba1-45">[43]</a></sup> about 840,000 acres (340,000 ha) of crops were flooded overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cuba2_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cuba2-46">[44]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Old_Havana" title="Old Havana">Old Havana</a>, mudslides severely damaged three homes and impacted numerous others to a lesser degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> The storm also produced heavy rainfall over portions of Florida, resulting in flooding in some areas, especially <a href="/wiki/Hillsborough_County,_Florida" title="Hillsborough County, Florida">Hillsborough</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pinellas_County,_Florida" title="Pinellas County, Florida">Pinellas</a> counties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColinTCR_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-ColinTCR-24">[22]</a></sup> There, the freshwater flooding was compounded by coastal flooding from high tides. Winds caused over 93,300 power outages throughout the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carr_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-carr-49">[47]</a></sup> The storm spawned at least one tornado, which knocked down trees and damaged several cars and homes in <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColinTCR_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-ColinTCR-24">[22]</a></sup> Four fatalities occurred in the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Panhandle" title="Florida Panhandle">Florida Panhandle</a> due to drowning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> Heavy rainfall was also observed in portions of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, North Carolina, and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>. Two additional drowning deaths occurred in Georgia and one in <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>. Damage throughout the East Coast reached $1.04 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sd6_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-sd6-51">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_Storm_Danielle">Tropical Storm Danielle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Tropical Storm Danielle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 24em;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight: bold; background: #00faf4">Tropical storm (SSHWS)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="vertical-align: middle"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Danielle_2016-06-20_1940Z.jpg"><img alt="Danielle 2016-06-20 1940Z.jpg" data-file-height="6481" data-file-width="5329" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Danielle_2016-06-20_1940Z.jpg/150px-Danielle_2016-06-20_1940Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Danielle_2016-06-20_1940Z.jpg/225px-Danielle_2016-06-20_1940Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Danielle_2016-06-20_1940Z.jpg/300px-Danielle_2016-06-20_1940Z.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a><span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Danielle_2016_track.png"><img alt="Danielle 2016 track.png" data-file-height="1854" data-file-width="3000" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Danielle_2016_track.png/150px-Danielle_2016_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Danielle_2016_track.png/225px-Danielle_2016_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Danielle_2016_track.png/300px-Danielle_2016_track.png 2x" width="150"/></a></span></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>June 19 – June 21</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Peak intensity</th>
<td>45 mph (75 km/h) <small>(1-min)</small>  1007 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>A tropical wave emerged off the western coast of Africa on June 8, reaching the southwestern Caribbean Sea by June 15. Convection increased that day, and further organized after the system entered the Bay of Campeche three days later, subsequently leading to the formation of a tropical depression by 12:00 UTC on June 19. Steered west-northwest and then northwest by a mid-level ridge, the depression intensified into Tropical Storm Danielle by 06:00 UTC on June 20 and attained peak winds of 45 mph (75 km/h) six hours later. Interaction with land began to weaken the storm a few hours later, and Danielle made landfall near <a href="/wiki/Tamiahua" title="Tamiahua">Tamiahua, Mexico</a> with winds of 40 mph (65 km/h). The storm rapidly weakened as it moved inland, falling to tropical depression intensity by 00:00 UTC on June 21 and degenerating into a remnant low six hours later. The remnant low continued inland before dissipating over the mountains of eastern Mexico that same day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DTCR_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-DTCR-52">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_warnings_and_watches" title="Tropical cyclone warnings and watches">tropical storm warning</a> was issued along the coast of Mexico from Laguna Verde to Rio Paranuco. It was later discontinued when Danielle moved ashore and rapidly weakened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DTR_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-DTR-53">[51]</a></sup> Danielle dropped heavy rainfall across the affected regions, particularly the areas near Veracruz and Tamiahua. Official amounts were not reported, however the maximum rainfall that was observed was around 6.22 in (158 mm) at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cosault%C3%A1n_de_Carvajal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cosaultán de Carvajal (page does not exist)">Cosaultán de Carvajal</a>. The cyclone also brought wind gusts up to 45 mph (72 km/h), reported near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tampico,_Mexico" title="Tampico, Mexico">Tampico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DTR_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-DTR-53">[51]</a></sup> Across much of Veracruz, officials suspended school activities and the port of Veracruz was temporarily closed. Flooding in the <a href="/wiki/Pueblo_Viejo_Municipality,_Veracruz" title="Pueblo Viejo Municipality, Veracruz">Pueblo Viejo Municipality</a> affected 1,200 families and prompted activation of public shelters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeless" title="Homeless">homeless</a> man drowned in a storm drain in <a href="/wiki/Ciudad_Madero" title="Ciudad Madero">Ciudad Madero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamaulipas" title="Tamaulipas">Tamaulipas</a>, after flash flooding impacted the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hurricane_Earl">Hurricane Earl</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Hurricane Earl">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 24em;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight: bold; background: #ffffcc">Category 1 hurricane (SSHWS)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="vertical-align: middle"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earl_2016-08-03_1950Z.jpg"><img alt="Earl 2016-08-03 1950Z.jpg" data-file-height="3023" data-file-width="2142" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Earl_2016-08-03_1950Z.jpg/150px-Earl_2016-08-03_1950Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Earl_2016-08-03_1950Z.jpg/225px-Earl_2016-08-03_1950Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Earl_2016-08-03_1950Z.jpg/300px-Earl_2016-08-03_1950Z.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a><span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earl_2016_track.png"><img alt="Earl 2016 track.png" data-file-height="1854" data-file-width="3000" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Earl_2016_track.png/150px-Earl_2016_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Earl_2016_track.png/225px-Earl_2016_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Earl_2016_track.png/300px-Earl_2016_track.png 2x" width="150"/></a></span></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>August 2 – August 6</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Peak intensity</th>
<td>85 mph (140 km/h) <small>(1-min)</small>  979 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Earl_(2016)" title="Hurricane Earl (2016)">Hurricane Earl (2016)</a></div>
<p>On July 26, a tropical wave emerged into the Atlantic from the west coast of Africa. The disturbance's rapid movement prevented significant development for several days. By August 2, a reconnaissance aircraft reported a closed circulation and tropical storm-force winds. As a result, the disturbance became Tropical Storm Earl around 06:00 UTC. Steered generally westward by a ridge over the South United States, Earl intensified amid warm ocean temperatures and low shear, attaining hurricane intensity and peaking with winds of 85 mph (140 km/h) on August 3. Earl struck <a href="/wiki/Turneffe_Atoll" title="Turneffe Atoll">Turneffe Caye</a> in Belize around 04:00 UTC on August 4 and then made landfall just south of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Belize_City,_Belize" title="Belize City, Belize">Belize City</a> about two hours later. It quickly weakened over land, but emerged into the Bay of Campeche on August 5 as a minimal tropical storm. A Hurricane Hunters mission flew into Earl later on August 5, measuring 60 mph (95 km/h) winds. The cyclone made landfall at that intensity near <a href="/wiki/Veracruz_(city)" title="Veracruz (city)">Veracruz, Veracruz</a>, around 02:00 UTC on August 6. Once inland, Earl quickly weakened, falling to tropical depression intensity at 12:00 UTC and dissipating by 18:00 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EarlTCR_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-EarlTCR-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The precursor to Earl brought heavy rain and gusty winds to the Lesser Antilles and Greater Antilles. Strong winds in the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> downed a power line onto a bus, subsequently causing a fire that killed six people. A boat crash in <a href="/wiki/Saman%C3%A1_Bay" title="Samaná Bay">Samaná Bay</a> killed seven people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weather_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-weather-56">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> Significant impacts were reported in Belize after Earl moved ashore as a hurricane, including downed trees and power lines, blown transformers, damaged or ripped-off roofs, coastal and inland flooding, and a significant storm surge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sitrep1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-sitrep1-58">[56]</a></sup> About 2,000 homes were damaged or destroyed throughout Belize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Reporter_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Reporter-59">[57]</a></sup> Damage in the country reached about $110 million. In Mexico, flooding and landslides resulted in severe damage and many deaths, especially in <a href="/wiki/Puebla" title="Puebla">Puebla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">Veracruz</a>. In the former, mudslides damaged hundreds of homes, including 350 in the village of Chicahuaxtla alone. <a href="/wiki/Huauchinango" title="Huauchinango">Huauchinango</a> observed a month's worth of rainfall in only about 24 hours, resulting in mudslides that killed at least 13 people. Throughout Puebla, there were at least 41 deaths. In Veracruz, Earl and the resultant landslides damaged about 6,300 homes and 26 roadways, while 13 fatalities occurred. Overall, Earl left $132 million in damage and 81 fatalities in Mexico, with 10 other people missing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EarlTCR_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-EarlTCR-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_Storm_Fiona">Tropical Storm Fiona</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tropical Storm Fiona">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 24em;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight: bold; background: #00faf4">Tropical storm (SSHWS)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="vertical-align: middle"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fiona_2016-08-20_1610Z.jpg"><img alt="Fiona 2016-08-20 1610Z.jpg" data-file-height="5208" data-file-width="4008" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Fiona_2016-08-20_1610Z.jpg/150px-Fiona_2016-08-20_1610Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Fiona_2016-08-20_1610Z.jpg/225px-Fiona_2016-08-20_1610Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Fiona_2016-08-20_1610Z.jpg/300px-Fiona_2016-08-20_1610Z.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a><span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fiona_2016_track.png"><img alt="Fiona 2016 track.png" data-file-height="1854" data-file-width="3000" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Fiona_2016_track.png/150px-Fiona_2016_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Fiona_2016_track.png/225px-Fiona_2016_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Fiona_2016_track.png/300px-Fiona_2016_track.png 2x" width="150"/></a></span></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>August 16 – August 23</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Peak intensity</th>
<td>50 mph (85 km/h) <small>(1-min)</small>  1004 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Late on August 14, the NHC began monitoring a tropical wave and its associated convection off the west coast of Africa for potential development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> Steered northwest toward a weakness in the subtropical ridge over the central Atlantic, the wave organized sufficiently to become a tropical depression by 18:00 UTC on August 16. The depression slowly organized after formation and developed of a central dense overcast feature, with the system becoming Tropical Storm Fiona by 12:00 UTC on August 17.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FionaTCR_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FionaTCR-61">[59]</a></sup> Despite strong westerly shear, abundant mid-level dry air, and an otherwise disheveled satellite appearance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fdisc17_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-fdisc17-62">[60]</a></sup> an <a href="/wiki/Scatterometer" title="Scatterometer">advanced scatterometer</a> (ASCAT) pass indicated a maximum sustained wind speed of 50 mph (85 km/h) early on August 19.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FionaTCR_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FionaTCR-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fdisc17_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-fdisc17-62">[60]</a></sup> Although sporadic bursts of convection continued amid the hostile environment, Fiona weakened to a tropical depression by 06:00 UTC on August 22 and degenerated into a remnant low early on August 23 about 375 mi (605 km) south-southwest of Bermuda. The remnants merged with a weakening frontal zone near Bermuda on August 25.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FionaTCR_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-FionaTCR-61">[59]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hurricane_Gaston">Hurricane Gaston</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Hurricane Gaston">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 24em;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight: bold; background: #ffc140">Category 3 hurricane (SSHWS)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="vertical-align: middle"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gaston_2016-08-30_1625Z.jpg"><img alt="Gaston 2016-08-30 1625Z.jpg" data-file-height="5208" data-file-width="4008" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Gaston_2016-08-30_1625Z.jpg/150px-Gaston_2016-08-30_1625Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Gaston_2016-08-30_1625Z.jpg/225px-Gaston_2016-08-30_1625Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Gaston_2016-08-30_1625Z.jpg/300px-Gaston_2016-08-30_1625Z.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a><span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gaston_2016_track.png"><img alt="Gaston 2016 track.png" data-file-height="2602" data-file-width="3000" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Gaston_2016_track.png/150px-Gaston_2016_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Gaston_2016_track.png/225px-Gaston_2016_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Gaston_2016_track.png/300px-Gaston_2016_track.png 2x" width="150"/></a></span></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>August 22 – September 2</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Peak intensity</th>
<td>120 mph (195 km/h) <small>(1-min)</small>  955 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On August 17, the NHC highlighted the potential for tropical cyclogenesis off the western coast of Africa in subsequent days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> A weak area of low pressure associated with a tropical wave emerged into the eastern Atlantic three days later,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> and the disturbance steadily coalesced into a tropical depression by 12:00 UTC on August 22, about 305 mi (490 km) southwest of the southernmost Cabo Verde Islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GastonTCR_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-GastonTCR-27">[25]</a></sup> The depression organized while heading northwest, intensifying into Tropical Storm Gaston six hours later and attaining hurricane intensity by 12:00 UTC on August 24, in accordance with data from satellites and an NASA <a href="/wiki/Northrop_Grumman_RQ-4_Global_Hawk" title="Northrop Grumman RQ-4 Global Hawk">Global Hawk</a> unmanned aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GastonTCR_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-GastonTCR-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> After its initial peak in intensity, Gaston's satellite appearance began to degrade as an upper-level low imparted strong southwesterly shear on the cyclone,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> causing it to weaken back to a tropical storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GastonTCR_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-GastonTCR-27">[25]</a></sup> Upper-level winds slackened early on August 27, and a timely microwave pass highlighted the presence of a low-level eye well embedded in the storm's central dense overcast, indicating the resumption of Gaston's intensification phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Gaston continued northwestward, its motion slowed in a weak steering regime. Amid low shear and warm ocean temperatures, Gaston attained hurricane intensity for a second time at 18:00 UTC on August 27. About 24 hours later, the storm deepened to a Category 3 hurricane on the <a href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_scale" title="Saffir–Simpson scale">Saffir–Simpson scale</a>, the first major hurricane of the season, by 18:00 UTC the next day. With a symmetric ring of deep convection surrounding a distinct eye, Gaston ultimately peaked with sustained winds of 120 mph (195 km/h) six hours later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GastonTCR_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-GastonTCR-27">[25]</a></sup> A mid-level trough moving southeastward across the North Atlantic eroded a series of ridges steering Gaston, causing the system to drift north and northeast. Cold water upwelling and an <a href="/wiki/Eyewall_replacement_cycle" title="Eyewall replacement cycle">eyewall replacement cycle</a> caused Gaston to weaken on August 29,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup> although the cyclone unexpectedly re-intensified to 120 mph (195 km/h) for a second time around 00:00 UTC on August 31. Later that day, it began encountering increasingly cool waters and a higher shear, leading the storm to fall below major hurricane status by 18:00 UTC on August 31, and below hurricane intensity by 12:00 UTC on September 2. Continuous unfavorable conditions caused deep convection to dissipate, and Gaston transitioned into an <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">extratropical cyclone</a> as its center grazed <a href="/wiki/Flores_Island_(Azores)" title="Flores Island (Azores)">Flores Island</a> at 18:00 UTC. Gaston's remnant circulation dissipated 24 hours later northwest of the Azores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GastonTCR_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-GastonTCR-27">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_Depression_Eight">Tropical Depression Eight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Tropical Depression Eight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 24em;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight: bold; background: #5ebaff">Tropical depression (SSHWS)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="vertical-align: middle"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:08L_2016-08-29_1825Z.jpg"><img alt="08L 2016-08-29 1825Z.jpg" data-file-height="3208" data-file-width="2408" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/08L_2016-08-29_1825Z.jpg/150px-08L_2016-08-29_1825Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/08L_2016-08-29_1825Z.jpg/225px-08L_2016-08-29_1825Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/08L_2016-08-29_1825Z.jpg/300px-08L_2016-08-29_1825Z.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a><span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:08-L_2016_track.png"><img alt="08-L 2016 track.png" data-file-height="1854" data-file-width="3000" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/08-L_2016_track.png/150px-08-L_2016_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/08-L_2016_track.png/225px-08-L_2016_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/08-L_2016_track.png/300px-08-L_2016_track.png 2x" width="150"/></a></span></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>August 28 – September 1</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Peak intensity</th>
<td>35 mph (55 km/h) <small>(1-min)</small>  1010 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>A broad low pressure developed on August 26 as a frontal boundary stalled near Bermuda and weakened. Early on August 27, the low became well-defined, but lacked sufficient convection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EightTCR_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-EightTCR-69">[67]</a></sup> and was plagued by unfavorable conditions such as dry air and moderate wind shear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> However, a large burst of convection near and to the west of the center prompted the upgrade to Tropical Depression Eight at 12:00 UTC on August 28 about 350 mi (560 km) southeast of <a href="/wiki/Cape_Hatteras" title="Cape Hatteras">Cape Hatteras</a>, North Carolina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EightTCR_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-EightTCR-69">[67]</a></sup> A ridge to the north steered the depression westward into an area of moderate wind shear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disc1_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-disc1-71">[69]</a></sup> Late on August 28, the center became exposed from the convection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup> but convection increased again after about 24 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d86_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-d86-73">[71]</a></sup> As it approached the Carolina coastline on August 30, a weakness in the subtropical ridge caused the depression to slow down and turn northward; its closest approach to the United States was 60 mi (95 km) south-southeast of Cape Hatteras later that day. The depression then turned eastward in response to becoming entangled in the mid-latitude <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">westerlies</a>. As it accelerated, the circulation began to become elongated. The depression degenerated into a <a href="/wiki/Trough_(meteorology)" title="Trough (meteorology)">trough</a> of low pressure by 00:00 UTC on September 1. The remnants were absorbed into a frontal system on September 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EightTCR_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-EightTCR-69">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The precursor low dropped about 1.4 in (36 mm) of rainfall on Bermuda, triggering some localized flooding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BWS_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-BWS-74">[72]</a></sup> As the depression approached the coastline, a tropical storm watch was issued for <a href="/wiki/Cape_Lookout_(North_Carolina)" title="Cape Lookout (North Carolina)">Cape Lookout</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oregon_Inlet,_North_Carolina" title="Oregon Inlet, North Carolina">Oregon Inlet, North Carolina</a>, early on August 29. This was later upgraded to a warning with the addition of including the <a href="/wiki/Pamlico_Sound" title="Pamlico Sound">Pamlico Sound</a>. As the depression failed to intensify and moved away from the coastline, the warnings were discontinued at 00:00 UTC on August 31.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EightTCR_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-EightTCR-69">[67]</a></sup> There were only reports of some rainfall, gusty winds, and minor surf in the Outer Banks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weather-2_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-weather-2-75">[73]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hurricane_Hermine">Hurricane Hermine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Hurricane Hermine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 24em;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight: bold; background: #ffffcc">Category 1 hurricane (SSHWS)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="vertical-align: middle"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hermine_2016-09-01_1625Z.jpg"><img alt="Hermine 2016-09-01 1625Z.jpg" data-file-height="9400" data-file-width="7200" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Hermine_2016-09-01_1625Z.jpg/150px-Hermine_2016-09-01_1625Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Hermine_2016-09-01_1625Z.jpg/225px-Hermine_2016-09-01_1625Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Hermine_2016-09-01_1625Z.jpg/300px-Hermine_2016-09-01_1625Z.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a><span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hermine_2016_track.png"><img alt="Hermine 2016 track.png" data-file-height="1887" data-file-width="3000" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Hermine_2016_track.png/150px-Hermine_2016_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Hermine_2016_track.png/225px-Hermine_2016_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Hermine_2016_track.png/300px-Hermine_2016_track.png 2x" width="150"/></a></span></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>August 28 – September 3</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Peak intensity</th>
<td>80 mph (130 km/h) <small>(1-min)</small>  981 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Hermine" title="Hurricane Hermine">Hurricane Hermine</a></div>
<p>A tropical wave emerged into the Atlantic from the west coast of Africa between August 16 and August 17. The wave failed to develop for several days due to its quick movement and dry air. Deep convection eventually consolidated and a circulation finally developed by August 28, with a tropical depression developing at 18:00 UTC, about 60 mi (100 km) south-southeast of <a href="/wiki/Key_West,_Florida" title="Key West, Florida">Key West, Florida</a>. Initially, the depression moved westward, until a break in a ridge caused it to move northeastward early on August 31. Around that time, the cyclone intensified into Tropical Storm Hermine. The storm steadily intensified into an 80 mph (130 km/h) Category 1 hurricane by early on September 2,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HermineTCR_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-HermineTCR-28">[26]</a></sup> becoming the first hurricane to form in the Gulf of Mexico since <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Ingrid" title="Hurricane Ingrid">Ingrid</a> in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> Hermine made landfall near <a href="/wiki/St._Marks,_Florida" title="St. Marks, Florida">St. Marks, Florida</a>, at 05:30 UTC. After moving inland, Hermine quickly weakened and transitioned into an extratropical cyclone on September 3 near the Outer Banks of North Carolina. The remnant system meandered offshore the Northeastern United States before dissipating over southeastern Massachusetts on September 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HermineTCR_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-HermineTCR-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The precursor system dropped heavy precipitation in portions of the Caribbean, especially the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> and Cuba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HermineTCR_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-HermineTCR-28">[26]</a></sup> In the former, the storm damaged more than 200 homes and displaced more than 1,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> Although some areas of Cuba recorded over 12 in (300 mm) of rain, the precipitation was generally beneficial due to a severe drought. In Florida, abnormally high tides and heavy precipitation along the gulf coast caused significant damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HermineTCR_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-HermineTCR-28">[26]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Citrus_County,_Florida" title="Citrus County, Florida">Citrus County</a>, one of the worst areas impacted, 2,694 structures were damaged, of which 531 experienced severe damage, while damage reached about $102 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> Similar coastal and freshwater flooding occurred in <a href="/wiki/Pasco_County,_Florida" title="Pasco County, Florida">Pasco County</a>, where 7 homes were destroyed, 305 sustained major damage, and 1,564 received minor damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> Winds primarily left power outages and downed trees, some of which fell onto buildings and vehicles. About 325,000 people were left without electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> Near <a href="/wiki/Ocala,_Florida" title="Ocala, Florida">Ocala</a>, a tree fell on a homeless man's tent, killing him. Flooding and fairly strong winds in other states such as Georgia, South Carolina, and North Carolina caused additional damage, but to a lesser extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HermineTCR_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-HermineTCR-28">[26]</a></sup> One death each occurred in South Carolina and North Carolina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HermineTCR_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-HermineTCR-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Herald_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Herald-81">[79]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, two fishermen drowned near the <a href="/wiki/Wading_River,_New_York" title="Wading River, New York">Wading River</a> on <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a> due to rough surf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> Overall, Hermine caused about $550 million in damage in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HermineTCR_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-HermineTCR-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_Storm_Ian">Tropical Storm Ian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2016_Atlantic_hurricane_season&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Tropical Storm Ian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 24em;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight: bold; background: #00faf4">Tropical storm (SSHWS)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="vertical-align: middle"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ian_2016-09-13_1645Z.jpg"><img alt="Ian 2016-09-13 1645Z.jpg" data-file-height="7800" data-file-width="6000" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Ian_2016-09-13_1645Z.jpg/150px-Ian_2016-09-13_1645Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Ian_2016-09-13_1645Z.jpg/225px-Ian_2016-09-13_1645Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Ian_2016-09-13_1645Z.jpg/300px-Ian_2016-09-13_1645Z.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a><span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ian_2016_track.png"><img alt="Ian 2016 track.png" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="2700" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ian_2016_track.png/150px-Ian_2016_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ian_2016_track.png/225px-Ian_2016_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ian_2016_track.png/300px-Ian_2016_track.png 2x" width="150"/></a></span></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>September 12 – September 16</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Peak intensity</th>
<td>60 mph (95 km/h) <small>(1-min)</small>  994 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On September 5, the NHC indicated that the development of a tropical cyclone was possible in the East Atlantic over subsequent days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> A tropical wave emerged off the west coast of Africa on September 6, slowly coalescing into Tropical Storm Ian by 06:00 UTC on September 12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IanTCR_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-IanTCR-84">[82]</a></sup> Steered north and the northeast by an approaching upper-level trough, the cyclone struggled within an environment of high shear, with its low-level center displaced west of its associated convection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> An upper-trough became superimposed with the storm's center by late on September 14, yielding a more subtropical-like appearance on conventional satellite.
